	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Gothic architecture</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Gothic style" redirects here. For the visual arts, see <a href="/wiki/Gothic_art" title="Gothic art">Gothic art</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Gothic church" redirects here. For the church of the Goths in the Early Middle Ages, see <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Christianity" title="Gothic Christianity">Gothic Christianity</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Style of architecture</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

architecture</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Gothic architecture</b> (Latin: <i>francigenum opus</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Architectural_style" title="Architectural style">style</a> that flourished in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> during the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High</a> and <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late</a> <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. It evolved from <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque architecture</a> and was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_architecture" title="Renaissance architecture">Renaissance architecture</a>. Originating in 12th-century <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, it was widely used, especially for cathedrals and churches, until the 16th century.
</p><p>Its most prominent features included the use of the <a href="/wiki/Rib_vault" title="Rib vault">rib vault</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Flying_buttress" title="Flying buttress">flying buttress</a>, which allowed the weight of the roof to be counterbalanced by buttresses outside the building, giving greater height and more space for windows.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDucher198846_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDucher198846-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Another important feature was the extensive use of <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">stained glass</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Rose_window" title="Rose window">rose window</a>, to bring light and color to the interior. Another feature was the use of realistic statuary on the exterior, particularly over the portals, to illustrate biblical stories for the largely illiterate parishioners. Some key architectural features, such as the <a href="/wiki/Pointed_arch" class="mw-redirect" title="Pointed arch">pointed arch</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rib_vault" title="Rib vault">rib vault</a>, were adopted from outside Europe, probably deriving from <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic architecture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughesPunterSmith201538_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughesPunterSmith201538-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiesePawlakThome2018149_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiesePawlakThome2018149-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> These features had both existed in <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque architecture</a>, but they were used more extensively and in more innovative ways to make Gothic cathedrals higher, stronger, and filled with light.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBechmann2017178–188_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBechmann2017178–188-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first important example is generally considered to be the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Saint-Denis" title="Basilica of Saint-Denis">Basilica of Saint-Denis</a>, near Paris, whose choir was reconstructed with Gothic <a href="/wiki/Rib_vault" title="Rib vault">rib vaults</a> and large stained glass windows between 1140 and 1144.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMignon20158–9_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMignon20158–9-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gothic architecture was known during the period as <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">opus francigenum</i> ("French/<a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Frankish</a> work").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones199990_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones199990-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The term "Gothic architecture" originated in the 16th century and was originally very negative, suggesting something barbaric. <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Vasari" title="Giorgio Vasari">Giorgio Vasari</a> used the term "barbarous German style" in his 1550 <i><a href="/wiki/Lives_of_the_Most_Excellent_Painters,_Sculptors,_and_Architects" title="Lives of the Most Excellent Painters, Sculptors, and Architects">Lives of the Artists</a></i> to describe what is now considered the Gothic style,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVasari1991117,_527_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVasari1991117,_527-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and in the introduction to the <i>Lives</i> he attributed various architectural features to "<a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">the Goths</a>" whom he held responsible for destroying the ancient buildings after they conquered <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, and erecting new ones in this style.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVasariBrownMaclehose190783_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVasariBrownMaclehose190783-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Origins_.E2.80.93_Early_Gothic_.281130.E2.80.931200.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_–_Early_Gothic_(1130–1200)">Origins – Early Gothic (1130–1200)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origins – Early Gothic (1130–1200)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>The Gothic style originated in the <a href="/wiki/Ile-de-France" class="mw-redirect" title="Ile-de-France">Ile-de-France</a> region of northern France in the first half of the 12th century. A new dynasty of French Kings, the <a href="/wiki/Capetians" class="mw-redirect" title="Capetians">Capetians</a>, had subdued the feudal lords, and had become the most powerful rulers in France, with their capital in Paris. They allied themselves with the bishops of the major cities of northern France, and reduced the power of the feudal abbots and monasteries. Their rise coincided with an enormous growth of the population and prosperity of the cities of northern France. The Capetian Kings and their bishops wished to build new cathedrals as monuments of their power, wealth, and religious faith.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980102_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980102-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The church which served as the primary model for the style was the <a href="/wiki/Abbey_of_St-Denis" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbey of St-Denis">Abbey of St-Denis</a>, which underwent reconstruction by the Abbot <a href="/wiki/Suger" title="Suger">Suger</a>, first in the choir and then the facade (1140–44), Suger was a close ally and biographer of the French King, <a href="/wiki/Louis_VII_of_France" title="Louis VII of France">Louis VII</a>, who was a fervent Catholic and builder, and the founder of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a>. Suger remodeled the ambulatory of the Abbey, removed the enclosures that separated the chapels, and replaced the existing structure with imposing pillars and <a href="/wiki/Rib_vault" title="Rib vault">rib vaults</a>. This created higher and wider bays, into which he installed larger windows, which filled the end of the church with light. Soon afterwards he rebuilt the facade, adding three deep portals, each with a <a href="/wiki/Tympanum_(architecture)" title="Tympanum (architecture)">tympanum</a>, an arch filled with sculpture illustrating biblical stories. The new facade was flanked by two towers. He also installed a small circular <a href="/wiki/Rose_window" title="Rose window">rose window</a> over the central portal. This design became the prototype for a series of new French cathedrals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartindale199317–18_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartindale199317–18-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sens_Cathedral" title="Sens Cathedral">Sens Cathedral</a> (begun between 1135 and 1140) was the first Cathedral to be built in the new style (St. Denis was an abbey, not a Cathedral). Other versions of the new style soon appeared in <a href="/wiki/Noyon_Cathedral" title="Noyon Cathedral">Noyon Cathedral</a> (begun 1150); <a href="/wiki/Laon_Cathedral" title="Laon Cathedral">Laon Cathedral</a> (begun 1165); and the most famous of all, <a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_Paris" title="Notre-Dame de Paris">Notre-Dame de Paris</a>, where construction had begun in 1160.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartindale199320–22_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartindale199320–22-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Gothic style was also adapted by some French monastic orders, notably the <a href="/wiki/Cistercian" class="mw-redirect" title="Cistercian">Cistercian</a> order under <a href="/wiki/Saint_Bernard_of_Clairvaux" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Bernard of Clairvaux">Saint Bernard of Clairvaux</a> It was used in an austere form without ornament at the new Cistercian <a href="/wiki/Abbey_of_Fontenay" title="Abbey of Fontenay">Abbey of Fontenay</a> (1139–1147) and the church of <a href="/wiki/Clairvaux_Abbey" title="Clairvaux Abbey">Clairvaux Abbey</a>, whose site is now occupied by a French prison.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartindale199324–26_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartindale199324–26-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The new style was also copied outside the Kingdom of France in the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Normandy" title="Duchy of Normandy">Duchy of Normandy</a>. Early examples of Norman Gothic included <a href="/wiki/Coutances_Cathedral" title="Coutances Cathedral">Coutances Cathedral</a> (1210–1274); <a href="/wiki/Bayeux_Cathedral" title="Bayeux Cathedral">Bayeux Cathedral</a> (rebuilt from Romanesque style in 12th century), <a href="/wiki/Le_Mans_Cathedral" title="Le Mans Cathedral">Le Mans Cathedral</a> (rebuilt from Romanesque 12th century) and <a href="/wiki/Rouen_Cathedral" title="Rouen Cathedral">Rouen Cathedral</a>. Through the rule of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Plantagenet" title="House of Plantagenet">Angevin dynasty</a>, the new style was introduced to <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and spread from there to <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDucher198846_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDucher198846-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197736_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197736-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The Gothic style did not immediately replace the Romanesque everywhere in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980102_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980102-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The Late Romanesque continued to flourish in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> under the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Hohenstaufen" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Hohenstaufen">Hohenstaufens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">Rhineland</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="High_Gothic_.281200.E2.80.931270.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="High_Gothic_(1200–1270)">High Gothic (1200–1270)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: High Gothic (1200–1270)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>From the end of the 12th century until the middle of the 13th century, the Gothic style spread from the Île-de-France to appear in other cities of northern France. New structures in the style included <a href="/wiki/Chartres_Cathedral" title="Chartres Cathedral">Chartres Cathedral</a> (begun 1200); <a href="/wiki/Bourges_Cathedral" title="Bourges Cathedral">Bourges Cathedral</a> (1195 to 1230), <a href="/wiki/Reims_Cathedral" title="Reims Cathedral">Reims Cathedral</a> (1211–1275), and <a href="/wiki/Amiens_Cathedral" title="Amiens Cathedral">Amiens Cathedral</a> (begun 1250);<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDucher198848_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDucher198848-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> At Chartres, the use of the flying buttresses allowed the elimination of the tribune level, which allowed much higher arcades and nave, and larger windows. The early type of <a href="/wiki/Rib_vault" title="Rib vault">rib vault</a> used of Saint Denis and Notre Dame, with six parts, was modified to four parts, making it simpler and stronger. Amiens and Chartres were among the first to use the <a href="/wiki/Flying_buttress" title="Flying buttress">flying buttress</a>; the buttresses were strengthened by an additional arch and with a supporting arcade, allowing even higher and walls and more windows. At Reims, the buttresses were given greater weight and strength by the addition of heavy stone pinnacles on top. These were often decorated with statues of angels, and became an important decorative element of the High Gothic style. Another practical and decorative element, the gargoyle, appeared; it was an ornamental rain spout which channeled the water from the roof away from the building. At Amiens, the windows of the nave were made larger, and an additional row of clear glass windows (the claire-voie) flooded the interior with light. The new structural technologies allowed the enlargement of the transepts and of the choirs at the east end of the cathedrals, creating the space for a ring of well-lit chapels. The transept of Notre-Dame was rebuilt with the new technology, and two spectacular rose windows added.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDucher198848_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDucher198848-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Rayonnant_Gothic_.281250.E2.80.931370s.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rayonnant_Gothic_(1250–1370s)">Rayonnant Gothic (1250–1370s)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Rayonnant Gothic (1250–1370s)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 302px"><div style="width: 302px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 300px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sainte_Chapelle_Interior_Stained_Glass.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Sainte_Chapelle_Interior_Stained_Glass.jpg/450px-Sainte_Chapelle_Interior_Stained_Glass.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Sainte_Chapelle_Interior_Stained_Glass.jpg/675px-Sainte_Chapelle_Interior_Stained_Glass.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Sainte_Chapelle_Interior_Stained_Glass.jpg/900px-Sainte_Chapelle_Interior_Stained_Glass.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5904" data-file-height="3936" /></a></div></div>

			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Columns of exterior framework supporting the windows of Sainte-Chapelle
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155.33333333333px"><div style="width: 155.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 153.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:North_rose_window_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris,_Aug_2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/North_rose_window_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris%2C_Aug_2010.jpg/230px-North_rose_window_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris%2C_Aug_2010.jpg" decoding="async" width="154" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/North_rose_window_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris%2C_Aug_2010.jpg/346px-North_rose_window_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris%2C_Aug_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/North_rose_window_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris%2C_Aug_2010.jpg/461px-North_rose_window_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris%2C_Aug_2010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3574" data-file-height="4650" /></a></div></div>

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 215.33333333333px"><div style="width: 215.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 213.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rose_du_transept_Sud_Notre-Dame_de_Paris_170208_04.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Rose_du_transept_Sud_Notre-Dame_de_Paris_170208_04.jpg/320px-Rose_du_transept_Sud_Notre-Dame_de_Paris_170208_04.jpg" decoding="async" width="214" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Rose_du_transept_Sud_Notre-Dame_de_Paris_170208_04.jpg/480px-Rose_du_transept_Sud_Notre-Dame_de_Paris_170208_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Rose_du_transept_Sud_Notre-Dame_de_Paris_170208_04.jpg/640px-Rose_du_transept_Sud_Notre-Dame_de_Paris_170208_04.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1587" data-file-height="1489" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Exterior of south rose window of <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Notre Dame Cathedral">Notre Dame Cathedral</a> (about 1250)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The next period was termed <i><a href="/wiki/Rayonnant" title="Rayonnant">Rayonnant</a></i> ("Radiant"), describing the tendency toward the use of more and more stained glass and less masonry in the design of the structure, until the walls seemed entirely made of glass. The most celebrated example was the chapel of <a href="/wiki/Sainte-Chapelle" title="Sainte-Chapelle">Sainte-Chapelle</a>, the chapel attached to the royal residence on the <a href="/wiki/Palais_de_la_Cit%C3%A9" title="Palais de la Cité">Palais de la Cité</a>. An elaborate system of exterior columns and arches reduced the walls of the upper chapel to a thin framework for the enormous windows. The weight of each of the masonry gables above the <a href="/wiki/Archivolt" title="Archivolt">archivolt</a> of the windows also helped the walls to resist the thrust and to distribute the weight.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDucher198858_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDucher198858-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another landmark of the Rayonnant Gothic are the two rose windows on the north and south of the transept of <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Notre Dame Cathedral">Notre Dame Cathedral</a>. Whereas earlier rose windows, like those of <a href="/wiki/Amiens_Cathedral" title="Amiens Cathedral">Amiens Cathedral</a>, were framed by stone and occupied only a portion of the wall, these two windows, with a delicate lace-like framework, occupied the entire space between the pillars.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDucher198858_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDucher198858-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Flamboyant_Gothic_.281350.E2.80.931550.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Flamboyant_Gothic_(1350–1550)">Flamboyant Gothic (1350–1550)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Flamboyant Gothic (1350–1550)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 152px"><div style="width: 152px">

			<div class="gallerytext">

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Flamboyant" title="Flamboyant">Flamboyant</a> Gothic style appeared in the second half of the 14th century. Its characteristic features were more exuberant decoration, as the nobles and wealthy citizens of mostly northern French cities competed to build more and more elaborate churches and cathedrals. It took its name from the sinuous, flame-like designs which ornamented windows. Other new features included the <i>arc en accolade</i>, a window decorated with an arch, stone pinnacles and floral sculpture. It also featured an increase in the number of <i>nervures</i>, or ribs, that supported and decorated each vault of the ceiling, both for greater support and decorative effect. Notable examples of Flamboyant Gothic include the western facade of <a href="/wiki/Rouen_Cathedral" title="Rouen Cathedral">Rouen Cathedral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sainte-Chapelle_de_Vincennes" title="Sainte-Chapelle de Vincennes">Sainte-Chapelle de Vincennes</a> in Paris, both built in the 1370s; and the Choir of <a href="/wiki/Mont_Saint_Michel_Abbey" class="mw-redirect" title="Mont Saint Michel Abbey">Mont Saint Michel Abbey</a> (1448 c.).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenaultLazé200637_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenaultLazé200637-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Subsequently, the style spread to Northern Europe. Notable example of Flamboyant Gothic in the Baltic region includes the <a href="/wiki/Brick_Gothic" title="Brick Gothic">Brick Gothic</a> <a href="/wiki/Church_of_St._Anne,_Vilnius" title="Church of St. Anne, Vilnius">Church of St. Anne</a> in <a href="/wiki/Vilnius" title="Vilnius">Vilnius</a> (1490s).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Elements_of_Gothic_style">Elements of Gothic style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Elements of Gothic style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plan">Plan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Plan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 121.33333333333px"><div style="width: 121.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 119.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Plan.cathedrale.Chartres.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Plan.cathedrale.Chartres.png/179px-Plan.cathedrale.Chartres.png" decoding="async" width="120" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Plan.cathedrale.Chartres.png/268px-Plan.cathedrale.Chartres.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Plan.cathedrale.Chartres.png/358px-Plan.cathedrale.Chartres.png 2x" data-file-width="556" data-file-height="932" /></a></div></div>

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 338px"><div style="width: 338px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 336px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cathedral_schematic_plan_en_vectorial.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Cathedral_schematic_plan_en_vectorial.svg/504px-Cathedral_schematic_plan_en_vectorial.svg.png" decoding="async" width="336" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Cathedral_schematic_plan_en_vectorial.svg/756px-Cathedral_schematic_plan_en_vectorial.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Cathedral_schematic_plan_en_vectorial.svg/1008px-Cathedral_schematic_plan_en_vectorial.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="910" data-file-height="542" /></a></div></div>

			<div class="thumb" style="width: 139.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coupe.nef.eglise.Saint.Denis.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Coupe.nef.eglise.Saint.Denis.png/209px-Coupe.nef.eglise.Saint.Denis.png" decoding="async" width="140" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Coupe.nef.eglise.Saint.Denis.png/313px-Coupe.nef.eglise.Saint.Denis.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Coupe.nef.eglise.Saint.Denis.png/417px-Coupe.nef.eglise.Saint.Denis.png 2x" data-file-width="663" data-file-height="953" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Cross section of the nave wall and buttresses of the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Saint_Denis" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilica of Saint Denis">Basilica of Saint Denis</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Viollet-le-Duc" class="mw-redirect" title="Eugene Viollet-le-Duc">Eugene Viollet-le-Duc</a>
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The plan of the Gothic cathedral was based on the model of the ancient Roman <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilica</a>, which was a combined public market and courthouse; which was also the basis of the plan of the Romanesque cathedral. The cathedral is in the form of a Latin cross. The entrance is traditionally on the west end, has three portals decorated with sculpture, usually a rose window, and is flanked by two towers. The long nave, where the congregation worshiped, occupies the west end. This is usually divided from the nave by rows of pillars, which support the roof, flanked by one or two aisles, called <i>collaérals</i>. There are usually small chapels on the two sides, placed between the buttresses, which provide additional support to the walls.
</p><p>The cathedral usually has a <a href="/wiki/Transept" title="Transept">transept</a>, a crossing, roughly in the middle, which sometimes projects outwards some distance, and in other cases, such as Notre-Dame, is minimal. The <i>croisée</i> or crossing of the transept, is the center of the church, and is surrounded by particularly massive pillars, which sometimes support a lantern tower, which brings light into the center of the cathedral. The north and south facades of the transept often feature rose windows, as at <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_de_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Notre Dame de Paris">Notre Dame de Paris</a>.
</p><p>To the east of the transept is the <a href="/wiki/Choir_(architecture)" title="Choir (architecture)">choir</a>, where the altar is located, where ceremonies take place, and where only the clergy was allowed. This space grew greatly in the 12th century, as ceremonies became more elaborate. Behind the choir is single or double a walkway called the <a href="/wiki/Ambulatory" title="Ambulatory">ambulatory</a>. At the eastern end of the church is the <a href="/wiki/Apse" title="Apse">apse</a> usually in the form of a half-circle, and the chevet. There is usually a chapel here dedicated to the Virgin Mary, which can be very large. Around chevet there are usually several other smaller chapels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWenzler201818–19_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWenzler201818–19-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The earlier Gothic cathedrals had four levels, from the floor to the roof. On the ground floor there were two rows grand arcades with large pillars, which received the weight of the vaults of the ceiling. Above these were the tribunes, a section of arched openings, giving more support. Above these was the <i>triforium</i>, a section of small arches. On the top level, just below the vaults, were the upper windows, the main source of light for the Cathedral. The lower walls were supported by massive <i>contreforts</i> or buttresses placed directly up against them, with pinnacles on top which provided additional weight.
</p><p>Later, with the development of the flying buttress, the supports moved further away from the walls, and the walls were built much higher. Gradually the tribunes and the triforium disappeared, and the walls above the arcades were occupied almost entirely with stained glass.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenaultLazé200633_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenaultLazé200633-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The eastern arm shows considerable diversity. In England it is generally long and may have two distinct sections, both choir and presbytery. It is often square ended or has a projecting <i>Lady Chapel</i>, dedicated to the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a>. In France the eastern end is often polygonal and surrounded by a walkway called an <a href="/wiki/Ambulatory" title="Ambulatory">ambulatory</a> and sometimes a ring of chapels called a "<a href="/wiki/Chevet" class="mw-redirect" title="Chevet">chevet</a>". While German churches are often similar to those of France, in Italy, the eastern projection beyond the transept is usually just a shallow apsidal chapel containing the sanctuary, as at <a href="/wiki/Florence_Cathedral" title="Florence Cathedral">Florence Cathedral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swaan_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swaan-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_rib_vault">The rib vault</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: The rib vault">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>In Romanesque architecture, the rounded arches of the <a href="/wiki/Barrel_vault" title="Barrel vault">barrel vaults</a> that covered the nave pressed directly down on the walls with crushing weight. This required massive columns, thick walls and small windows, and naturally limited the height of the building. Gothic architects found a solution through an innovative use of the <a href="/wiki/Rib_vault" title="Rib vault">rib vault</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDucher198846_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDucher198846-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMignon201510-11_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMignon201510-11-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An early kind of rib vault, used at <a href="/wiki/Mosque-Cathedral_of_Cordoba" class="mw-redirect" title="Mosque-Cathedral of Cordoba">Mosque-Cathedral of Cordoba</a> (8th century), was found under a cupola or dome, but was different in form and function from the later Gothic rib vault. The cupola itself was supported by <a href="/wiki/Pendentives" class="mw-redirect" title="Pendentives">pendentives</a> or <a href="/wiki/Squinches" class="mw-redirect" title="Squinches">squinches</a>, a practice used in <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine architecture</a>. The ribs were decorative. The vaults in churches in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> dated to the 11th century, after Sicily had been conquered by the Normans, and resembled the vaults used at the same time in Normandy and England.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBechmann2017188–190_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBechmann2017188–190-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gothic builders designed a new and lighter kind of rib vault. They divided into compartments by a diagonal crossing of thin stone ribs (<i>ogives</i>), and completed by two additional arcs perpendicular and parallel to the nave (<i>doubleaux</i> and <i>formerets</i>). They also made an innovative use of the broken arch or pointed arch. In Islamic and Romanesque architecture, pointed arches had usually been used in doorways and windows. Gothic architects used them at the meeting points of the ribs at the top of the vaults, which distributed the weight of the roof downwards and outwards, not just downwards.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBechmann2017183–185_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBechmann2017183–185-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> These ribs divided the early vaults into six compartments, each as wide as two traverses of the nave. Some of the ribs went downwards as colonettes, and were bundled into pillars on the ground floor. Other ribs carried the thrust outwards to the walls, where it was counterbalanced by heavy <a href="/wiki/Flying_buttresses" class="mw-redirect" title="Flying buttresses">flying buttresses</a> outside the walls. Since the weight was supported by pillars and buttresses, the walls themselves could be much higher and thinner. This made possible the expanses of <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">stained glass</a> that were a characteristic of Gothic cathedrals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDucher198846_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDucher198846-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMignon201510-11_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMignon201510-11-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBechmann2017183–185_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBechmann2017183–185-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The earlier Gothic rib vaults, used at Notre Dame, Noyon, and Laon, were divided by the ribs into six compartments, were very difficult to build, and could only cross a limited space. In later cathedral construction, the design was simplified, and the rib vaults had only four compartments, and could cover a wider span; a single vault could cross the nave, and fewer pillars were needed. The four-part vaults also made it possible to build the cathedrals even higher. <a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_Paris" title="Notre-Dame de Paris">Notre-Dame de Paris</a>, begun in 1163 with four-part vaults, reached a height of 35 meters, remarkable for the time. <a href="/wiki/Amiens_Cathedral" title="Amiens Cathedral">Amiens Cathedral</a>, begun in 1220 with four-part ribs, reached the height of 42.30 meters (138.8 feet) at the transept.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDucher198846_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDucher198846-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMignon201518-28_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMignon201518-28-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the later period of the Gothic style, from the early 14th century, the rib vaults lost their elegant simplicity, and were loaded with additional ribs, sculptural designs, and sometimes pendants and other purely decorative elements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDucher198846_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDucher198846-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flying_buttress">Flying buttress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Flying buttress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 138.66666666667px"><div style="width: 138.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 136.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coupe.transversale.cathedrale.Paris.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Coupe.transversale.cathedrale.Paris.png/205px-Coupe.transversale.cathedrale.Paris.png" decoding="async" width="137" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Coupe.transversale.cathedrale.Paris.png/308px-Coupe.transversale.cathedrale.Paris.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Coupe.transversale.cathedrale.Paris.png/410px-Coupe.transversale.cathedrale.Paris.png 2x" data-file-width="716" data-file-height="1047" /></a></div></div>

</p>

		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 169.33333333333px"><div style="width: 169.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 167.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arc.boutant.cathedrale.Amiens.2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Arc.boutant.cathedrale.Amiens.2.png/251px-Arc.boutant.cathedrale.Amiens.2.png" decoding="async" width="168" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Arc.boutant.cathedrale.Amiens.2.png/377px-Arc.boutant.cathedrale.Amiens.2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Arc.boutant.cathedrale.Amiens.2.png/502px-Arc.boutant.cathedrale.Amiens.2.png 2x" data-file-width="505" data-file-height="603" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The flying buttresses at Amiens combined carefully balanced weight with decoration (drawing by <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Viollet-le-Duc" class="mw-redirect" title="Eugene Viollet-le-Duc">Eugene Viollet-le-Duc</a>)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Another important feature of Gothic architecture was the <a href="/wiki/Flying_buttress" title="Flying buttress">flying buttress</a>, designed to support the walls by means of arches connected to counter-supports outside the walls. Flying buttresses had existed in simple forms since Roman times, but the Gothic builders raised their use to a fine art, balancing the thrust from the roof inside against the counter-thrust of the buttresses. The earliest Gothic cathedrals, including Saint-Denis and Notre-Dame in its beginning stages, did not have flying buttresses. Their walls were supported by heavy stone abutments placed directly against the walls, The roof was supported by the ribs of the vaults, which were bundled with the columns below.
</p><p>In the later 12th and early 13th century, the buttresses became more sophisticated. New arches carried the thrust of the weight entirely outside the walls, where it was met by the counter-thrust of stone columns, with pinnacles placed on top for decoration and for additional weight. Thanks to this system of external buttresses, the walls could be higher and thinner, and could support larger stained glass windows. The buttresses themselves became part of the decoration; the pinnacles became more and more ornate, becoming more and more elaborate, as at <a href="/wiki/Beauvais_Cathedral" title="Beauvais Cathedral">Beauvais Cathedral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reims_Cathedral" title="Reims Cathedral">Reims Cathedral</a>. The arches had an additional practical purpose; they contained lead channels which carried rain water off the roof; it was expelled from the mouths of stone <a href="/wiki/Gargoyles" class="mw-redirect" title="Gargoyles">gargoyles</a> placed in rows on the buttresses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDucher198850–51_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDucher198850–51-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late Gothic periods the buttresses became extremely ornate, with a large amount of non-functional decoration in the form of pinnacles, curving arches, counter-curves, statuary and ornamental pendants.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Height">Height</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Height">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

			<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Nave" title="Nave">Nave</a> of <a href="/wiki/Milan_Cathedral" title="Milan Cathedral">Milan Cathedral</a> 45&#160;m (148&#160;ft) high (1389–1452)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>An important characteristic of Gothic church architecture is its height, both absolute and in proportion to its width, the verticality suggesting an aspiration to Heaven. The increasing height of cathedrals over the Gothic period was accompanied by an increasing proportion of the wall devoted to windows, until, by the late Gothic, the interiors became like cages of glass. This was made possible by the development of the flying buttress, which transferred the thrust of the weight of the roof to the supports outside the walls. As a result, the walls gradually became thinner and higher, and masonry was replaced with glass. The four-part elevation of the naves of early Cathedrals such as Notre-Dame (arcade, tribune, triforium, claire-voie) was transformed in the choir of <a href="/wiki/Beauvais_Cathedral" title="Beauvais Cathedral">Beauvais Cathedral</a> to very tall arcades, a thin triforium, and soaring windows up to the roof.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWenzler2018108_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWenzler2018108-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beauvais Cathedral reached the limit of what was possible with Gothic technology. A portion of the choir collapsed in 1284, causing alarm in all of the cities with very tall cathedrals. Panels of experts were created in Sienna and Chartres to study the stability of those structures.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartindale199386_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartindale199386-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Only the transept and choir of Beauvais were completed, and in the 21st century the transept walls were reinforced with cross-beams. No cathedral built since exceeded the height of the choir of Beauvais.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWenzler2018108_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWenzler2018108-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A section of the main body of a Gothic church usually shows the <a href="/wiki/Nave" title="Nave">nave</a> as considerably taller than it is wide. In England the proportion is sometimes greater than 2:1, while the greatest proportional difference achieved is at <a href="/wiki/Cologne_Cathedral" title="Cologne Cathedral">Cologne Cathedral</a> with a ratio of 3.6:1. The highest internal vault is at <a href="/wiki/Beauvais_Cathedral" title="Beauvais Cathedral">Beauvais Cathedral</a> at 48 metres (157&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-BF_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235.33333333333px"><div style="width: 235.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 233.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Canterbury_Cathedral,_south_Oculus_(37582928966).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Canterbury_Cathedral%2C_south_Oculus_%2837582928966%29.jpg/350px-Canterbury_Cathedral%2C_south_Oculus_%2837582928966%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="234" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Canterbury_Cathedral%2C_south_Oculus_%2837582928966%29.jpg/525px-Canterbury_Cathedral%2C_south_Oculus_%2837582928966%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Canterbury_Cathedral%2C_south_Oculus_%2837582928966%29.jpg/700px-Canterbury_Cathedral%2C_south_Oculus_%2837582928966%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3341" data-file-height="2864" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>South <a href="/wiki/Oculus" title="Oculus">Oculus</a> of <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">Canterbury Cathedral</a>. It contains fourteen of the original glass sections from the 12th century
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gothic-Rayonnant_Rose-6.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gothic-Rayonnant_Rose-6.jpg/300px-Gothic-Rayonnant_Rose-6.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gothic-Rayonnant_Rose-6.jpg/450px-Gothic-Rayonnant_Rose-6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gothic-Rayonnant_Rose-6.jpg/600px-Gothic-Rayonnant_Rose-6.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3574" data-file-height="3574" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Rayonnant" title="Rayonnant">Rayonnant</a> north <a href="/wiki/Rose_window" title="Rose window">rose window</a> of <a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_Paris" title="Notre-Dame de Paris">Notre-Dame de Paris</a> (about 1250)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>One of the most prominent features of Gothic architecture was the use of stained glass window, which steadily grew in height and size and filled cathedrals with light and color. Historians including Viollet-le-Duc, Focillon, Aubert, and <a href="/wiki/Max_Dvo%C5%99%C3%A1k" title="Max Dvořák">Max Dvořák</a> contended that this is one of the most universal features of the Gothic style.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197720_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197720-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Religious teachings in the Middle Ages, particularly the writings of Religious <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Dionysius" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudo-Dionysius">Pseudo-Dionysius</a>, a 6th-century mystic whose book, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Celestial_Hierarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="The Celestial Hierarchy">The Celestial Hierarchy</a></i>, was popular among monks in France, taught that all light was divine. When the Abbot <a href="/wiki/Suger" title="Suger">Suger</a> ordered the reconstruction of the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Saint_Denis" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilica of Saint Denis">Basilica of Saint Denis</a>, he instructed that the windows in the choir admit as much light as possible.
</p><p>Many earlier Romanesque churches had stained glass windows, and many had round windows, called <i>oculi</i>, but these windows were necessarily small, due to the thickness of the walls. The primary interior decorations of Romanesque cathedrals were painted murals. In the Gothic period, the improvements in rib vaults and flying buttresses allowed Cathedral walls to be higher, thinner and stronger, and windows were consequently considerably larger, The windows of churches in the late Gothic period, such as <a href="/wiki/Sainte_Chapelle" class="mw-redirect" title="Sainte Chapelle">Sainte Chapelle</a> in Paris, filled the entire wall between the ribs of stone. Enormous windows were also an important element of <a href="/wiki/York_Minster" title="York Minster">York Minster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gloucester_Cathedral" title="Gloucester Cathedral">Gloucester Cathedral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Swaan_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swaan-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The main threat to cathedral windows was the wind; frames had to be extremely strong. The early windows were fit into openings cut into the stone. The small pieces of colored glass were joined together with pieces of lead, and then their surfaces were painted with faces and other details. and then the windows were mounted in the stone frames. Thin vertical and horizontal bars of iron, called <i>vergettes</i> or <i>barlotierres</i>, were placed inside the window to reinforce the glass.
</p><p>The stories told in the glass were usually episodes from the Bible, but they also sometimes illustrated the professions of the guilds which had funded the windows, such as the drapers, stonemasons or the barrel-makers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWenzler201828_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWenzler201828-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Much of the stained glass in Gothic cathedrals today dates from later restorations, but a few cathedrals, notably <a href="/wiki/Chartres_Cathedral" title="Chartres Cathedral">Chartres Cathedral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bourges_Cathedral" title="Bourges Cathedral">Bourges Cathedral</a>, still have many of their original windows.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWenzler201828_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWenzler201828-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Portals_and_the_tympanum">Portals and the tympanum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Portals and the tympanum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 152px"><div style="width: 152px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saint_Denis_PM_086190_F.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Saint_Denis_PM_086190_F.jpg/225px-Saint_Denis_PM_086190_F.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Saint_Denis_PM_086190_F.jpg/337px-Saint_Denis_PM_086190_F.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b0/Saint_Denis_PM_086190_F.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="600" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>North portal of <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_St_Denis" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilica of St Denis">Basilica of St Denis</a>, with early <a href="/wiki/Tympanum_(architecture)" title="Tympanum (architecture)">tympanum</a> and columns made of elongated figures (1135–1140)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 268.66666666667px"><div style="width: 268.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 266.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amiens_cathedral_Tympanumt.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Amiens_cathedral_Tympanumt.JPG/400px-Amiens_cathedral_Tympanumt.JPG" decoding="async" width="267" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Amiens_cathedral_Tympanumt.JPG/600px-Amiens_cathedral_Tympanumt.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2a/Amiens_cathedral_Tympanumt.JPG 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Day of Judgement <a href="/wiki/Tympanum_(architecture)" title="Tympanum (architecture)">tympanum</a> at <a href="/wiki/Amiens_Cathedral" title="Amiens Cathedral">Amiens Cathedral</a> (1220–1270), the prototype for other high Gothic portals.
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 268.66666666667px"><div style="width: 268.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 266.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frontispice_cath%C3%A9drale_Strasbourg.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Frontispice_cath%C3%A9drale_Strasbourg.JPG/400px-Frontispice_cath%C3%A9drale_Strasbourg.JPG" decoding="async" width="267" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Frontispice_cath%C3%A9drale_Strasbourg.JPG/600px-Frontispice_cath%C3%A9drale_Strasbourg.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Frontispice_cath%C3%A9drale_Strasbourg.JPG/800px-Frontispice_cath%C3%A9drale_Strasbourg.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Portals and tympanum of <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg_Cathedral" title="Strasbourg Cathedral">Strasbourg Cathedral</a> (Begun 1176)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 131.33333333333px"><div style="width: 131.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 129.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:WestminsterNorth55.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/WestminsterNorth55.jpg/194px-WestminsterNorth55.jpg" decoding="async" width="130" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/WestminsterNorth55.jpg/291px-WestminsterNorth55.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/WestminsterNorth55.jpg/389px-WestminsterNorth55.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3704" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> north portal (begun 1245)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Early Gothic Cathedrals traditionally have their main entrance at the western end of the church, opposite the choir. Based on the model of the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Saint_Denis" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilica of Saint Denis">Basilica of Saint Denis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_Paris" title="Notre-Dame de Paris">Notre-Dame de Paris</a>, there are usually three doorways with pointed arches, richly filled with sculpture. The <a href="/wiki/Tympanum_(architecture)" title="Tympanum (architecture)">tympanum</a>, or arch, over each doorway is filled with realistic statues illustrating biblical stories, and the columns between the doors are often also crowded with statuary. Following the example of Amiens, the tympanum over the central portal traditionally depicted the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgement" class="mw-redirect" title="Last Judgement">Last Judgement</a>, the right portal showed the coronation of the Virgin Mary, and the left portal showed the lives of saints who were important in the diocese.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenaultLazé200635_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenaultLazé200635-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The iconography of the sculptural decoration on the facade was not left to the artists. An edict of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Nicaea" title="Second Council of Nicaea">Second Council of Nicaea</a> in 787 had set the rules: "The composition of religious images is not to be left to the inspiration of artists; it is derived from the principles put in place by the Catholic Church and religious tradition. Only the art belongs to the artist; the composition belongs to the Fathers."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWenzler201879_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWenzler201879-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The portals and interiors were much more colorful than they are today. Each sculpture on the tympanum and in the interior was painted by the <i>peintre imagier</i>, or image painter, following a system of colors codified in the 12th century; yellow, called <i>gold</i>, symbolized intelligence, grandeur and virtue; white, called <i>argent</i>, symbolized purity, wisdom, and correctness; black, or <i>sable</i>, meant sadness, but also will; green, or <i>sinopole</i>, represented hope, liberty and joy; red or <i>guelues</i> meant charity or victory; blue, or <i>azure</i> symbolized the sky, faithfulness and perseverance; and violet, or <i>pourpre</i>, was the color of royalty and sovereignty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWenzler201854_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWenzler201854-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Towers_and_spires">Towers and spires</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Towers and spires">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 304px"><div style="width: 304px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 302px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:La_cath%C3%A9drale_de_Laon_DSC_0707.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/La_cath%C3%A9drale_de_Laon_DSC_0707.jpg/453px-La_cath%C3%A9drale_de_Laon_DSC_0707.jpg" decoding="async" width="302" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/La_cath%C3%A9drale_de_Laon_DSC_0707.jpg/680px-La_cath%C3%A9drale_de_Laon_DSC_0707.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/La_cath%C3%A9drale_de_Laon_DSC_0707.jpg/906px-La_cath%C3%A9drale_de_Laon_DSC_0707.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4928" data-file-height="3264" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Laon_Cathedral" title="Laon Cathedral">Laon Cathedral</a>, with five towers (1160–1230)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 274.66666666667px"><div style="width: 274.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 272.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chevet_abbHommes.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Chevet_abbHommes.JPG/409px-Chevet_abbHommes.JPG" decoding="async" width="273" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Chevet_abbHommes.JPG/614px-Chevet_abbHommes.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Chevet_abbHommes.JPG/819px-Chevet_abbHommes.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2202" data-file-height="1614" /></a></div></div>

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 125.33333333333px"><div style="width: 125.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 123.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Basilique_Notre-Dame_de_l%27Epine.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Basilique_Notre-Dame_de_l%27Epine.JPG/185px-Basilique_Notre-Dame_de_l%27Epine.JPG" decoding="async" width="124" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Basilique_Notre-Dame_de_l%27Epine.JPG/278px-Basilique_Notre-Dame_de_l%27Epine.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Basilique_Notre-Dame_de_l%27Epine.JPG/371px-Basilique_Notre-Dame_de_l%27Epine.JPG 2x" data-file-width="730" data-file-height="1181" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Basilica of <a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_l%27%C3%89pine" title="Notre-Dame de l&#39;Épine">Notre-Dame de l'Épine</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Verdun" title="Verdun">Verdun</a>, (1420) an example of <a href="/wiki/Flamboyant_Gothic" class="mw-redirect" title="Flamboyant Gothic">Flamboyant Gothic</a> towers
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 126.66666666667px"><div style="width: 126.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 124.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rouen_Cathedral_as_seen_from_Gros_Horloge_140215_4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Rouen_Cathedral_as_seen_from_Gros_Horloge_140215_4.jpg/187px-Rouen_Cathedral_as_seen_from_Gros_Horloge_140215_4.jpg" decoding="async" width="125" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Rouen_Cathedral_as_seen_from_Gros_Horloge_140215_4.jpg/281px-Rouen_Cathedral_as_seen_from_Gros_Horloge_140215_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Rouen_Cathedral_as_seen_from_Gros_Horloge_140215_4.jpg/375px-Rouen_Cathedral_as_seen_from_Gros_Horloge_140215_4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3953" data-file-height="6328" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Towers of <a href="/wiki/Rouen_Cathedral" title="Rouen Cathedral">Rouen Cathedral</a> (15th and 16th centuries)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 244.66666666667px"><div style="width: 244.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 242.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:SalisburyCathedral-wyrdlight-EastExt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/SalisburyCathedral-wyrdlight-EastExt.jpg/364px-SalisburyCathedral-wyrdlight-EastExt.jpg" decoding="async" width="243" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/SalisburyCathedral-wyrdlight-EastExt.jpg/545px-SalisburyCathedral-wyrdlight-EastExt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/SalisburyCathedral-wyrdlight-EastExt.jpg/727px-SalisburyCathedral-wyrdlight-EastExt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="908" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Tower of <a href="/wiki/Salisbury_Cathedral" title="Salisbury Cathedral">Salisbury Cathedral</a>, the tallest in Britain (1220v1258)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The plan for the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Saint-Denis" title="Basilica of Saint-Denis">Basilica of Saint-Denis</a> called for two towers of equal height on the west facade, and this general plan was copied at Notre-Dame and most of the early cathedrals. The towers of <a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_Paris" title="Notre-Dame de Paris">Notre-Dame de Paris</a>, 69 meters (226&#160;ft) tall, were intended to be seen throughout the city; they were the tallest towers in Paris until the completion of the <a href="/wiki/Eiffel_Tower" title="Eiffel Tower">Eiffel Tower</a> in 1889. An informal but vigorous competition began in northern France for the tallest Cathedral towers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWenzler201895–98_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWenzler201895–98-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To make the churches taller and more prominent, and visible from a distance, heir builders often added a <i>flèche</i>, a spire usually made of wood and covered with lead, to the top of each tower, or, as in <a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_Paris" title="Notre-Dame de Paris">Notre-Dame de Paris</a>, in the center of the transept. Later in the Gothic period, more massive towers were constructed over the transept, rivaling or exceeding in height the towers of the facade.
</p><p>The towers were usually the last part of the Cathedral to be constructed. They were often built many years or decades after the rest of the building. Sometimes, by the time the towers were built, the plans had changed, or the money had run out. As a result, some Gothic cathedrals had just one tower, or two towers of different heights or styles. On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Laon_Cathedral" title="Laon Cathedral">Laon Cathedral</a>, begun just before Notre-Dame, boasted five towers; two on the facade, two on the transept, and a central lantern. An additional two were planned but not built. The <a href="/wiki/Abbey_of_Saint-%C3%89tienne,_Caen" title="Abbey of Saint-Étienne, Caen">Abbey of Saint-Étienne, Caen</a> originally built in the Romanesque style, was rebuilt with nine Gothic towers in the 13th century.
</p><p>The informal competition for the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_church_buildings" title="List of tallest church buildings">tallest church in Europe</a> went on throughout the Gothic period, sometimes with disastrous results. <a href="/wiki/Beauvais_Cathedral" title="Beauvais Cathedral">Beauvais Cathedral</a> had the tallest tower (153 meters or 502 feet), completed in 1569, for a brief time, until its tower collapsed in the wind in 1573. <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Cathedral" title="Lincoln Cathedral">Lincoln Cathedral</a> (159.7 meters or 524 feet) also had the record from 1311 until 1549 until its tower also collapsed. Today the tallest cathedral tower in France is <a href="/wiki/Rouen_Cathedral" title="Rouen Cathedral">Rouen Cathedral</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cologne_Cathedral" title="Cologne Cathedral">Cologne Cathedral</a> (151.0 meters or 495 feet) is the tallest cathedral in Europe.
</p><p>The Gothic <a href="/wiki/Old_St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="Old St Paul&#39;s Cathedral">Old St Paul's Cathedral</a> (1087–1314) had been the tallest cathedral in England until it was destroyed by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_London" title="Great Fire of London">Great Fire of London</a> in 1666. Today the tallest combined Gothic tower and spire in the UK belongs to <a href="/wiki/Salisbury_Cathedral" title="Salisbury Cathedral">Salisbury Cathedral</a>, (123 meters or 404 feet), built 1220–1258.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, the tower, if present, is almost always detached from the building, as at <a href="/wiki/Florence_Cathedral" title="Florence Cathedral">Florence Cathedral</a>, and is often from an earlier structure.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Florence cathedral is a pretty atypical example: it&#39;s not really medieval gothic, it&#39;s more rinascimental. And detaching the bell-tower from the church body is far from ordinary. (February 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, two towers on the front is the norm. In <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> this is often the arrangement, but an English cathedral may also be surmounted by an enormous tower at the crossing. Smaller churches usually have just one tower, but this may also be the case at larger buildings, such as <a href="/wiki/Salisbury_Cathedral" title="Salisbury Cathedral">Salisbury Cathedral</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ulm_Minster" title="Ulm Minster">Ulm Minster</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ulm" title="Ulm">Ulm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, completed in 1890 and possessing the tallest spire in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOggins200080_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOggins200080-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> slightly exceeding that of <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Cathedral" title="Lincoln Cathedral">Lincoln Cathedral</a>, the tallest spire that was actually completed during the medieval period, at 160 metres (520&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sculpture_and_decoration">Sculpture and decoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Sculpture and decoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>The exteriors and interiors of Gothic cathedrals, particularly in France, were lavishly ornamented with sculpture and decoration on religious themes, designed for the great majority of parishioners who could not read. They were described as "Books for the poor." To add to the effect, all of the sculpture on the facades was originally painted and gilded.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Each feature of the Cathedral had a symbolic meaning. The main portals at <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_de_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Notre Dame de Paris">Notre Dame de Paris</a>, for instance, represented the entrance to paradise, with the <a href="/wiki/Last_judgement" class="mw-redirect" title="Last judgement">last judgement</a> depicted on the <a href="/wiki/Tympanum_(architecture)" title="Tympanum (architecture)">tympanum</a> over the doors, showing Christ surrounded by the apostles, and by the signs of the zodiac, representing the movements of the heavens. The columns below the tympanum are in the form of statues of saints, literally reprinting them as "the pillars of the church."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNamara2017158–59_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNamara2017158–59-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Each Saint had his own symbol; a winged lion stood for <a href="/wiki/Saint_Mark" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Mark">Saint Mark</a>; an eagle with four wings meant <a href="/wiki/Saint_John_the_Apostle" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint John the Apostle">Saint John the Apostle</a>, and a winged bull symbolized <a href="/wiki/Saint_Luke" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Luke">Saint Luke</a>. Sculpted angels had specific functions, sometimes as heralds, blowing trumpets, or holding up columns, as guardian angels; or holding crowns of thorns or crosses, as symbols of the crucifixion of Christ, or waving a container with incense, to illustrate theirfunction at the throne of God. Floral and vegetal decoration was also very common, representing the Garden of Eden; grapes represented the wines of <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNamara2017158–59_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNamara2017158–59-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tympanum_(architecture)" title="Tympanum (architecture)">tympanum</a> over the central portal on the west facade of <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_de_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Notre Dame de Paris">Notre Dame de Paris</a> vividly illustrates the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgement" class="mw-redirect" title="Last Judgement">Last Judgement</a>, with figures of sinners being led off to hell, and good Christians taken to heaven. The sculpture of the right portal shows the coronation of the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a>, and the left portal shows the lives of saints who were important to Parisians, particularly <a href="/wiki/Saint_Anne" title="Saint Anne">Saint Anne</a>, the mother of the Virgin Mary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenaultLazé200635_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenaultLazé200635-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The exteriors of cathedrals and other Gothic churches were also decorated with sculptures of a variety of fabulous and frightening <a href="/wiki/Grotesque_(architecture)" title="Grotesque (architecture)">grotesques</a> or monsters. These included the <a href="/wiki/Chimera_(mythology)" title="Chimera (mythology)">chimera</a>, a mythical hybrid creature which usually had the body of a lion and the head of a goat, and the <a href="/wiki/Strix_(mythology)" title="Strix (mythology)">Strix</a> or stryge, a creature resembling an <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owl</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bat</a>, which was said to eat human flesh. The <i>strix</i> appeared in classical Roman literature; it was described by the Roman poet <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>, who was widely read in the Middle Ages, as a large-headed bird with transfixed eyes, rapacious beak, and greyish white wings.<sup id="cite_ref-fasti_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fasti-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> They were part of the visual message for the illiterate worshipers, symbols of the evil and danger that threatened those who did not follow the teachings of the church.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWenzler201897–99_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWenzler201897–99-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Gargoyle" title="Gargoyle">gargoyles</a>, which were added to Notre Dame in about 1240, had a more practical purpose. They were the rain spouts of the cathedral, designed to divide the torrent of water which poured from the roof after rain, and to project it outwards as far as possible from the buttresses and the walls and windows so that it would not erode the mortar binding the stone. To produce many thin streams rather than a torrent of water, a large number of gargoyles were used, so they were also designed to be a decorative element of the architecture. The rainwater ran from the roof into lead gutters, then down channels on the flying buttresses, then along a channel cut in the back of the gargoyle and out of the mouth away from the cathedral.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many of the statues, particularly the grotesques, were removed from facade in the 17th and 18th century, or were destroyed during the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>. They were replaced with figures in the Gothic style, designed by <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Viollet-le-Duc" class="mw-redirect" title="Eugene Viollet-le-Duc">Eugene Viollet-le-Duc</a>, during the 19th century restoration. Similar figures appear on the other Gothic Cathedrals of France.
</p><p>Another common feature of Gothic cathedrals in France was a <a href="/wiki/Labyrinth" title="Labyrinth">labyrinth</a> or maze on the floor of the nave near the choir, which symbolized the difficult and often complicated journey of a Christian life before attaining paradise. Most labyrinths were removed by the 18th century, but a few, like the one at <a href="/wiki/Amiens_Cathedral" title="Amiens Cathedral">Amiens Cathedral</a>, have been reconstructed, and the labyrinth at <a href="/wiki/Chartres_Cathedral" title="Chartres Cathedral">Chartres Cathedral</a> still exists essentially in its original form.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWenzler201899–100_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWenzler201899–100-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="French_Gothic">French Gothic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: French Gothic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/French_Gothic_architecture" title="French Gothic architecture">French Gothic architecture</a></div>

		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 271.33333333333px"><div style="width: 271.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 269.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nef_de_la_cath%C3%A9drale_d%27Auxerre.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Nef_de_la_cath%C3%A9drale_d%27Auxerre.jpg/404px-Nef_de_la_cath%C3%A9drale_d%27Auxerre.jpg" decoding="async" width="270" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Nef_de_la_cath%C3%A9drale_d%27Auxerre.jpg/606px-Nef_de_la_cath%C3%A9drale_d%27Auxerre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Nef_de_la_cath%C3%A9drale_d%27Auxerre.jpg/808px-Nef_de_la_cath%C3%A9drale_d%27Auxerre.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3438" data-file-height="2554" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Interior of <a href="/wiki/Auxerre_Cathedral" title="Auxerre Cathedral">Auxerre Cathedral</a> in Burgundy (1215–1233)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 264.66666666667px"><div style="width: 264.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 262.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Albi_Sainte-C%C3%A9cile.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Albi_Sainte-C%C3%A9cile.JPG/394px-Albi_Sainte-C%C3%A9cile.JPG" decoding="async" width="263" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Albi_Sainte-C%C3%A9cile.JPG/591px-Albi_Sainte-C%C3%A9cile.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Albi_Sainte-C%C3%A9cile.JPG/788px-Albi_Sainte-C%C3%A9cile.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3693" data-file-height="2812" /></a></div></div>
