		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 209.33333333333px"><div style="width: 209.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 207.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:-0.16666666666667px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:St_Pancras_Railway_Station_2012-06-23.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/St_Pancras_Railway_Station_2012-06-23.jpg/311px-St_Pancras_Railway_Station_2012-06-23.jpg" decoding="async" width="208" height="176" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/St_Pancras_Railway_Station_2012-06-23.jpg/467px-St_Pancras_Railway_Station_2012-06-23.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/St_Pancras_Railway_Station_2012-06-23.jpg/623px-St_Pancras_Railway_Station_2012-06-23.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8466" data-file-height="7140" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/St_Pancras_railway_station" title="St Pancras railway station">St Pancras railway station</a>, London
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>In England, partly in response to a philosophy propounded by the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Movement" title="Oxford Movement">Oxford Movement</a> and others associated with the emerging revival of 'high church' or <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Catholic">Anglo-Catholic</a> ideas during the second quarter of the 19th century, neo-Gothic began to become promoted by influential establishment figures as the preferred style for ecclesiastical, civic and institutional architecture. The appeal of this <a href="/wiki/Gothic_revival" class="mw-redirect" title="Gothic revival">Gothic revival</a> (which after 1837, in Britain, is sometimes termed <a href="/wiki/Victorian_Gothic" class="mw-redirect" title="Victorian Gothic">Victorian Gothic</a>), gradually widened to encompass "low church" as well as "high church" clients. This period of more universal appeal, spanning 1855–1885, is known in Britain as <a href="/wiki/High_Victorian_Gothic" title="High Victorian Gothic">High Victorian Gothic</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Houses of Parliament</a> in London by Sir <a href="/wiki/Charles_Barry" title="Charles Barry">Charles Barry</a> with interiors by a major exponent of the early Gothic Revival, <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Welby_Pugin" class="mw-redirect" title="Augustus Welby Pugin">Augustus Welby Pugin</a>, is an example of the Gothic revival style from its earlier period in the second quarter of the 19th century. Examples from the <i>High Victorian Gothic</i> period include <a href="/wiki/George_Gilbert_Scott" title="George Gilbert Scott">George Gilbert Scott</a>'s design for the <a href="/wiki/Albert_Memorial" title="Albert Memorial">Albert Memorial</a> in London, and <a href="/wiki/William_Butterfield" title="William Butterfield">William Butterfield</a>'s chapel at <a href="/wiki/Keble_College,_Oxford" title="Keble College, Oxford">Keble College, Oxford</a>. From the second half of the 19th century onwards, it became more common in Britain for neo-Gothic to be used in the design of non-ecclesiastical and non-governmental buildings types. Gothic details even began to appear in working-class housing schemes subsidised by philanthropy, though given the expense, less frequently than in the design of upper and middle-class housing.
</p><p>The middle of the 19th century was a period marked by the restoration, and in some cases modification, of ancient monuments and the construction of neo-Gothic edifices such as the nave of <a href="/wiki/Cologne_Cathedral" title="Cologne Cathedral">Cologne Cathedral</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sainte-Clotilde,_Paris" title="Sainte-Clotilde, Paris">Sainte-Clotilde</a> of <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> as speculation of medieval architecture turned to technical consideration. London's Palace of Westminster, St. Pancras railway station, New York's Trinity Church and St. Patrick's Cathedral are also famous examples of Gothic Revival buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Such style also reached the <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far East</a> in the period, for instance, the <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglican</a> <a href="/wiki/St._John%27s_Cathedral_(Hong_Kong)" title="St. John&#39;s Cathedral (Hong Kong)">St. John's Cathedral</a> which was located at the centre of <a href="/wiki/Victoria,_Hong_Kong" title="Victoria, Hong Kong">Victoria City</a> in <a href="/wiki/Central,_Hong_Kong" title="Central, Hong Kong">Central, Hong Kong</a>.
</p><p>While some credit for this new ideation can reasonably be assigned to German and English writers, namely <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Vetter" title="Johannes Vetter">Johannes Vetter</a>, Franz Mertens, and Robert Willis respectively, this emerging style's champion was <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Viollet-le-Duc" title="Eugène Viollet-le-Duc">Eugène Viollet-le-Duc</a>, whose lead was taken by archaeologists, historians, and architects like <a href="/wiki/Jules_Quicherat" title="Jules Quicherat">Jules Quicherat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Choisy" title="Auguste Choisy">Auguste Choisy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Aubert" title="Marcel Aubert">Marcel Aubert</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197711_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197711-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In the last years of the 19th century, a trend among study in art history emerged in Germany that a building, as defined by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Focillon" title="Henri Focillon">Henri Focillon</a> was an interpretation of space.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197713_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197713-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> When applied to Gothic cathedrals, historians and architects used to the dimensions of 17th and 18th <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture" title="Baroque architecture">Baroque</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">Neoclassical</a> structures, were astounded by the height and extreme length of the cathedrals compared to its proportionally modest width. <a href="/wiki/Goethe" class="mw-redirect" title="Goethe">Goethe</a>, in the preceding century, was mesmerised by the space within a Gothic church and succeeding historians like <a href="/wiki/Georg_Dehio" title="Georg Dehio">Georg Dehio</a>, Walter Ueberwasser, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Frankl" title="Paul Frankl">Paul Frankl</a>, and Maria Velte sought to rediscover the methodology used in their construction by making measurements and drawings of the buildings, and reading and making conjectures from documents and treaties pertaining to their construction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197714_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197714-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gothic_revival_in_France">Gothic revival in France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Gothic revival in France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 134px"><div style="width: 134px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 132px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:P1020476_Paris_VII_Basilique_Saint-Clotilde_rwk.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/P1020476_Paris_VII_Basilique_Saint-Clotilde_rwk.JPG/198px-P1020476_Paris_VII_Basilique_Saint-Clotilde_rwk.JPG" decoding="async" width="132" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/P1020476_Paris_VII_Basilique_Saint-Clotilde_rwk.JPG/298px-P1020476_Paris_VII_Basilique_Saint-Clotilde_rwk.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/P1020476_Paris_VII_Basilique_Saint-Clotilde_rwk.JPG/397px-P1020476_Paris_VII_Basilique_Saint-Clotilde_rwk.JPG 2x" data-file-width="992" data-file-height="1498" /></a></div></div>

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 268.66666666667px"><div style="width: 268.66666666667px">

			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182px"><div style="width: 182px">

		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 144.66666666667px"><div style="width: 144.66666666667px">

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 134.66666666667px"><div style="width: 134.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 132.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:%C3%89glise_des_R%C3%A9form%C3%A9s_in_Marseille.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/%C3%89glise_des_R%C3%A9form%C3%A9s_in_Marseille.jpg/199px-%C3%89glise_des_R%C3%A9form%C3%A9s_in_Marseille.jpg" decoding="async" width="133" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/%C3%89glise_des_R%C3%A9form%C3%A9s_in_Marseille.jpg/299px-%C3%89glise_des_R%C3%A9form%C3%A9s_in_Marseille.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/%C3%89glise_des_R%C3%A9form%C3%A9s_in_Marseille.jpg/398px-%C3%89glise_des_R%C3%A9form%C3%A9s_in_Marseille.jpg 2x" data-file-width="680" data-file-height="1024" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/%C3%89glise_Saint-Vincent-de-Paul" title="Église Saint-Vincent-de-Paul">Church of Église Saint-Vincent-de-Paul</a>, Marseille (1855-86)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>France had an abundance of original Gothic architecture, and therefore Gothic restoration largely replaced revival; few new buildings appeared in the Gothic style.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> caused enormous damage to the Gothic architecture of France. <a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_Paris" title="Notre-Dame de Paris">Notre-Dame de Paris</a> and other cathedrals and churches were closed and stripped of much of their decoration. In the 1830s, under the new King, <a href="/wiki/Louis-Philippe" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis-Philippe">Louis-Philippe</a>, the historic and artistic value of Cathedrals and other Gothic monuments was recognized. Interest in the Gothic style was also stimulated by the huge success of the novel <i>Notre Dame de Paris</i> (known in English as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hunchback_of_Notre_Dame" class="mw-redirect" title="The Hunchback of Notre Dame">The Hunchback of Notre Dame</a></i>) by <a href="/wiki/Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo">Victor Hugo</a>, published in 1831. The writings of the romantic author <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois-Ren%C3%A9_de_Chateaubriand" title="François-René de Chateaubriand">François-René de Chateaubriand</a> also played an important part; he praised the religious sentiments inspired by of the Gothic style, and declaring that the Gothic style was "the true architecture of our country," as important in the history of France as the forests of ancient Gaul.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEToman200799_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEToman200799-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> A Commission of Historic Monuments was created in 1837, and major program of cataloging, protection and restoration of Gothic monuments was organized by the writer <a href="/wiki/Prosper_Merim%C3%A9e" class="mw-redirect" title="Prosper Merimée">Prosper Merimée</a> and conducted largely by the architect <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Viollet-le-Duc" title="Eugène Viollet-le-Duc">Eugène Viollet-le-Duc</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEToman200799_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEToman200799-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Viollet-le-Duc" class="mw-redirect" title="Viollet-le-Duc">Viollet-le-Duc</a> conducted the restoration of <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_de_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Notre Dame de Paris">Notre Dame de Paris</a> and many other churches, as well as the Gothic fortifications of <a href="/wiki/Carcassonne" title="Carcassonne">Carcassonne</a> and imaginatively finishing the castle of <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Pierrefonds" title="Château de Pierrefonds">Château de Pierrefonds</a>, which had been only a ruin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPoisson2014_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPoisson2014-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> He did not always limit himself to strict restoration of what had existed; in 1838 he designed he built a new Gothic west facade for the Parish church of Saint-Ouen in <a href="/wiki/Rouen" title="Rouen">Rouen</a>. The architects <a href="/wiki/Franz_Christian_Gau" title="Franz Christian Gau">Franz Christian Gau</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9odore_Ballu" title="Théodore Ballu">Théodore Ballu</a> built <a href="/wiki/Sainte-Clotilde,_Paris" title="Sainte-Clotilde, Paris">The Basilica of Sainte-Clotilde</a> (1846–57), the first entirely neo-Gothic church in Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEToman2007101_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEToman2007101-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany_and_Russia">Germany and Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Germany and Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 268.66666666667px"><div style="width: 268.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 266.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Woerlitzpark81.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Woerlitzpark81.jpg/400px-Woerlitzpark81.jpg" decoding="async" width="267" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Woerlitzpark81.jpg/600px-Woerlitzpark81.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Woerlitzpark81.jpg/800px-Woerlitzpark81.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Gothic garden cottage of <a href="/wiki/Leopold_III,_Duke_of_Anhalt-Dessau" title="Leopold III, Duke of Anhalt-Dessau">Leopold III, Duke of Anhalt-Dessau</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Dessau-W%C3%B6rlitz_Garden_Realm" title="Dessau-Wörlitz Garden Realm">Dessau-Wörlitz Garden Realm</a> in Germany (1785–1783)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 270px"><div style="width: 270px">

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 302px"><div style="width: 302px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 300px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tsaritsino_from_helicopter-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Tsaritsino_from_helicopter-1.jpg/450px-Tsaritsino_from_helicopter-1.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Tsaritsino_from_helicopter-1.jpg/675px-Tsaritsino_from_helicopter-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Tsaritsino_from_helicopter-1.jpg/900px-Tsaritsino_from_helicopter-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="667" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 270px"><div style="width: 270px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 268px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:AlexandriaCapella.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/AlexandriaCapella.jpg/402px-AlexandriaCapella.jpg" decoding="async" width="268" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/AlexandriaCapella.jpg/602px-AlexandriaCapella.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/AlexandriaCapella.jpg/803px-AlexandriaCapella.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1558" data-file-height="1164" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>St. Alexander Nevsky <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Chapel_(Peterhof)" title="Gothic Chapel (Peterhof)">Gothic Chapel (Peterhof)</a>, completed in 1834
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>German Gothic revival was born in 1785–86 in the <a href="/wiki/Dessau-W%C3%B6rlitz_Garden_Realm" title="Dessau-Wörlitz Garden Realm">Dessau-Wörlitz Garden Realm</a>, an early landscape garden built by near <a href="/wiki/Potsdam" title="Potsdam">Potsdam</a>, in the garden of the country house of <a href="/wiki/Leopold_III,_Duke_of_Anhalt-Dessau" title="Leopold III, Duke of Anhalt-Dessau">Leopold III, Duke of Anhalt-Dessau</a>, designed by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_von_Erdmannsdorff" title="Friedrich Wilhelm von Erdmannsdorff">Friedrich Wilhelm von Erdmannsdorff</a>. The <i>Gothic House</i> displayed his collection of medieval stained glass. It inspired other Gothic-style country houses near Berlin and Kassel.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEToman2007156_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEToman2007156-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Germany, the early 19th century Gothic revival had close ties to the movement of <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romanticism</a>. Beginning in 1814, the writer and historian <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Gorres" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Gorres">Joseph Gorres</a> launched a movement for the completion <a href="/wiki/Cologne_cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Cologne cathedral">Cologne cathedral</a>, which had been halted in 1473. The work was begun in 1842, and the two towers were completed in 1880, six hundred years after the cathedral was begun. Similar projects were undertaken for <a href="/wiki/Ulm_Minster" title="Ulm Minster">Ulm Minster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heilsbronn_Abbey" title="Heilsbronn Abbey">Heilsbronn Abbey</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Isartor" title="Isartor">Isartor</a> gate in Munich. The Baroque decoration of these monuments was removed, and they were returned to at least an approximation, sometimes romanticized, of their original Gothic appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEToman2007156_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEToman2007156-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most picturesque example of Gothic revival is <a href="/wiki/Neuschwanstein_Castle" title="Neuschwanstein Castle">Neuschwanstein Castle</a> (1869–1876) in Bavaria built by <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_II_of_Bavaria" title="Ludwig II of Bavaria">Ludwig II of Bavaria</a>. For inspiration, Ludwig visited other Gothic reconstruction projects, <a href="/wiki/Warburg" title="Warburg">Warburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Pierrefonds" title="Château de Pierrefonds">Château de Pierrefonds</a>, a castle which had been reconstructed by <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Viollet-le-Duc" class="mw-redirect" title="Eugene Viollet-le-Duc">Eugene Viollet-le-Duc</a>. The highly romantic Neuschwanstein Castle inspired, among other later buildings, the Sleeping Beauty Castle in <a href="/wiki/Disneyland" title="Disneyland">Disneyland</a> (1955).
</p><p>Though Russia had no tradition of Gothic architecture, The Empress <a href="/wiki/Catherine_the_Great" title="Catherine the Great">Catherine the Great</a> chose the style in <a href="/wiki/Tsaritsyno_Palace" title="Tsaritsyno Palace">Tsaritsyno Palace</a>, a large palace complex she constructed south of Moscow from 1776 until her death in 1796. Later, Czar <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_I_of_Russia" title="Nicholas I of Russia">Nicholas I of Russia</a> commissioned the German romantic writer and painter <a href="/wiki/Karl_Friedrich_Schinkel" title="Karl Friedrich Schinkel">Karl Friedrich Schinkel</a> to design a Gothic chapel for his park at <a href="/wiki/Peterhof_Palace" title="Peterhof Palace">Peterhof Palace</a> near St. Petersburg. It was completed in 1834.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Italy_2">Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 283.33333333333px"><div style="width: 283.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 281.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Milano_il_duomo_1735.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Milano_il_duomo_1735.gif/422px-Milano_il_duomo_1735.gif" decoding="async" width="282" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3a/Milano_il_duomo_1735.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="472" data-file-height="336" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Milan_Cathedral" title="Milan Cathedral">Milan Cathedral</a> in 1735, with its original facade
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 268px"><div style="width: 268px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 266px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Milan_Cathedral_from_Piazza_del_Duomo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Milan_Cathedral_from_Piazza_del_Duomo.jpg/399px-Milan_Cathedral_from_Piazza_del_Duomo.jpg" decoding="async" width="266" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Milan_Cathedral_from_Piazza_del_Duomo.jpg/599px-Milan_Cathedral_from_Piazza_del_Duomo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Milan_Cathedral_from_Piazza_del_Duomo.jpg/799px-Milan_Cathedral_from_Piazza_del_Duomo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4164" data-file-height="3129" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Milan_Cathedral" title="Milan Cathedral">Milan Cathedral</a> with new facade (1806–1813) and new crown on fronton (1888)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 268.66666666667px"><div style="width: 268.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 266.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Caff%C3%A8_Pedrocchi.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Caff%C3%A8_Pedrocchi.JPG/400px-Caff%C3%A8_Pedrocchi.JPG" decoding="async" width="267" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Caff%C3%A8_Pedrocchi.JPG/600px-Caff%C3%A8_Pedrocchi.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Caff%C3%A8_Pedrocchi.JPG/800px-Caff%C3%A8_Pedrocchi.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The Neo-Gothic <a href="/wiki/Pedrocchi_Caf%C3%A9" title="Pedrocchi Café">Pedrocchi Café</a> in <a href="/wiki/Padua" title="Padua">Padua</a>, Italy (1837)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The Gothic revival in Italy focused on the completion of earlier Cathedrals, which had been largely left to crumble. The most striking example was <a href="/wiki/Milan_Cathedral" title="Milan Cathedral">Milan Cathedral</a>, which received a new facade in 1806–1813 which almost completely covered the original; it was designed by Giuseppe Zanoia and <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Amati" title="Carlo Amati">Carlo Amati</a>. In 1888 it received a Gothic crown on the front facade by <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Brentano" title="Giuseppe Brentano">Giuseppe Brentano</a>. The famous spires and pinnacles of the building date to the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEToman2007123_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEToman2007123-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A small but notable example of Gothic revival in Italy is the <a href="/wiki/Pedrocchi_Caf%C3%A9" title="Pedrocchi Café">Pedrocchi Café</a> in Padua, Italy (1837), by <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Jappelli" title="Giuseppe Jappelli">Giuseppe Jappelli</a>. It borrowed the <a href="/wiki/Venetian_Gothic" class="mw-redirect" title="Venetian Gothic">Venetian Gothic</a> style plus the pinnacles of Milan Cathedral. It became famous as the meeting place of Italian patriots rebelling against Austrian rule in 1848.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival_architecture_in_Canada" title="Gothic Revival architecture in Canada">Gothic Revival architecture in Canada</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 302px"><div style="width: 302px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 300px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bas%C3%ADlica_de_Notre-Dame,_Montreal,_Canad%C3%A1,_2017-08-12,_DD_22-24_HDR.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Bas%C3%ADlica_de_Notre-Dame%2C_Montreal%2C_Canad%C3%A1%2C_2017-08-12%2C_DD_22-24_HDR.jpg/450px-Bas%C3%ADlica_de_Notre-Dame%2C_Montreal%2C_Canad%C3%A1%2C_2017-08-12%2C_DD_22-24_HDR.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Bas%C3%ADlica_de_Notre-Dame%2C_Montreal%2C_Canad%C3%A1%2C_2017-08-12%2C_DD_22-24_HDR.jpg/675px-Bas%C3%ADlica_de_Notre-Dame%2C_Montreal%2C_Canad%C3%A1%2C_2017-08-12%2C_DD_22-24_HDR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Bas%C3%ADlica_de_Notre-Dame%2C_Montreal%2C_Canad%C3%A1%2C_2017-08-12%2C_DD_22-24_HDR.jpg/900px-Bas%C3%ADlica_de_Notre-Dame%2C_Montreal%2C_Canad%C3%A1%2C_2017-08-12%2C_DD_22-24_HDR.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7730" data-file-height="5153" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_Basilica_(Montreal)" title="Notre-Dame Basilica (Montreal)">Notre Dame Basilica in Montreal</a> (1785–1795)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 269.33333333333px"><div style="width: 269.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 267.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Parliament-Ottawa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Parliament-Ottawa.jpg/401px-Parliament-Ottawa.jpg" decoding="async" width="268" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Parliament-Ottawa.jpg/601px-Parliament-Ottawa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Parliament-Ottawa.jpg/802px-Parliament-Ottawa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1153" data-file-height="863" /></a></div></div>

<p>Buildings of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Canada" title="Parliament of Canada">Parliament of Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a> (1859–1927) by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Fuller_(architect)" title="Thomas Fuller (architect)">Thomas Fuller</a>
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 174.66666666667px"><div style="width: 174.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 172.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ottawa_-_ON_-_Library_of_Parliament.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Ottawa_-_ON_-_Library_of_Parliament.jpg/259px-Ottawa_-_ON_-_Library_of_Parliament.jpg" decoding="async" width="173" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Ottawa_-_ON_-_Library_of_Parliament.jpg/388px-Ottawa_-_ON_-_Library_of_Parliament.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Ottawa_-_ON_-_Library_of_Parliament.jpg/518px-Ottawa_-_ON_-_Library_of_Parliament.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3234" data-file-height="3746" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Library of Canadian Parliament in <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a> (1859–1927) by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Fuller_(architect)" title="Thomas Fuller (architect)">Thomas Fuller</a>
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 213.33333333333px"><div style="width: 213.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 211.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:St-michaels-toronto.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/St-michaels-toronto.jpg/317px-St-michaels-toronto.jpg" decoding="async" width="212" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/08/St-michaels-toronto.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="466" data-file-height="441" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The Gothic Revival <a href="/wiki/St._Michael%27s_Cathedral,_Toronto" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Michael&#39;s Cathedral, Toronto">St. Michael's Cathedral</a> in Toronto, by <a href="/wiki/William_Thomas_(architect)" title="William Thomas (architect)">William Thomas</a> (1845–1848)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>One of the earliest and most highly decorated Gothic Revival churches in North America is the <a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_Basilica_(Montreal)" title="Notre-Dame Basilica (Montreal)">Notre-Dame Basilica in Montreal</a>. Built in 1784–1795, on the site of a 17th-century church. The vaults are colored deep blue and decorated with golden stars, and the rest of the <a href="/wiki/Sanctuary" title="Sanctuary">sanctuary</a> is decorated in blues, azures, reds, purples, silver, and gold. The stained glass windows depict scenes from the religious history of Montreal.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The English Gothic revival style was imported from England beginning in the 1830s, and was used primarily for Anglican and Catholic churches, and for university buildings. The best-known Gothic revival buildings in Canada are those <a href="/w/index.php?title=Centre_Bloc&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Centre Bloc (page does not exist)">Centre Bloc</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Tower_(Canada)" title="Victoria Tower (Canada)">Victoria Tower</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Canada" title="Parliament of Canada">Parliament of Canada</a> on <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Hill" title="Parliament Hill">Parliament Hill</a> in Ottawa, by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Fuller_(architect)" title="Thomas Fuller (architect)">Thomas Fuller</a> (1859–1927).<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The architect <a href="/wiki/William_Thomas_(architect)" title="William Thomas (architect)">William Thomas</a> constructed <a href="/wiki/St._Michael%27s_Cathedral,_Toronto" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Michael&#39;s Cathedral, Toronto">St. Michael's Cathedral</a> in Toronto (1845–48), and several other Gothic revival churches in the Province of <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>.
</p>

			<div class="thumb" style="width: 301.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Las_Vegas_(4583982142).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Las_Vegas_%284583982142%29.jpg/452px-Las_Vegas_%284583982142%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="302" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Las_Vegas_%284583982142%29.jpg/678px-Las_Vegas_%284583982142%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Las_Vegas_%284583982142%29.jpg/904px-Las_Vegas_%284583982142%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Excalibur_Hotel_and_Casino" title="Excalibur Hotel and Casino">Excalibur Hotel and Casino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas" title="Las Vegas">Las Vegas</a> (1990)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 277.33333333333px"><div style="width: 277.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 275.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brookings_Hall_at_Washington_University.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Brookings_Hall_at_Washington_University.jpg/413px-Brookings_Hall_at_Washington_University.jpg" decoding="async" width="276" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Brookings_Hall_at_Washington_University.jpg/620px-Brookings_Hall_at_Washington_University.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Brookings_Hall_at_Washington_University.jpg/826px-Brookings_Hall_at_Washington_University.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4845" data-file-height="3520" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Brookings_Hall" title="Brookings Hall">Brookings Hall</a> on the campus of <a href="/wiki/Washington_University_in_St._Louis" title="Washington University in St. Louis">Washington University in St. Louis</a> (1900-1902)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The Gothic style was entirely contrary to the early <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritan</a> architecture in the United States, which valued simplicity and a lack of ornament. It was also contrary to the Neoclassical style used for early U.S. government buildings. However, in the mid to late 19th century, large cathedrals and churches began to be built in the Gothic Revival style, modeled after European cathedrals. University buildings also adopted the style, using English colleges as a model. In the early 20th century, Gothic decorative elements appeared on the towers of the new skyscrapers, notably the <a href="/wiki/Woolworth_Building" title="Woolworth Building">Woolworth Building</a> by <a href="/wiki/Cass_Gilbert" title="Cass Gilbert">Cass Gilbert</a> in New York and the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune_Building" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicago Tribune Building">Chicago Tribune Building</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, by <a href="/wiki/John_Mead_Howells" title="John Mead Howells">John Mead Howells</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Hood" title="Raymond Hood">Raymond Hood</a>, symbolizing the role of these buildings as "cathedrals of commerce."<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gothic architecture also inspired a popular style of residential architecture in the United States in the mid-19th century. It was known as <a href="/wiki/Carpenter_Gothic" title="Carpenter Gothic">Carpenter Gothic</a>, and featured pointed <a href="/wiki/Lancet_windows" class="mw-redirect" title="Lancet windows">lancet windows</a>, a steep gable roof, and other simple Gothic elements, embellished with wooden ornament cut with a <a href="/wiki/Jigsaw_(tool)" title="Jigsaw (tool)">jigsaw</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Scroll_saw" title="Scroll saw">scroll saw</a> in Gothic designs. Sometimes the wooden trim was attached to a brick house. It became a popular style for wooden churches in rural communities. An example of Carpenter Gothic house, the <a href="/wiki/American_Gothic_House" title="American Gothic House">American Gothic House</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Eldon,_Iowa" title="Eldon, Iowa">Eldon, Iowa</a> appears in the background of the celebrated painting <i><a href="/wiki/American_Gothic" title="American Gothic">American Gothic</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Grant_Wood" title="Grant Wood">Grant Wood</a>, and gave the painting its name.<sup id="cite_ref-Slate_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slate-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ArtInstituteChicago_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ArtInstituteChicago-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Latin_America.2C_Asia_and_the_Pacific"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_America,_Asia_and_the_Pacific">Latin America, Asia and the Pacific</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Latin America, Asia and the Pacific">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Gothic_Revival_architecture" title="List of Gothic Revival architecture">List of Gothic Revival architecture</a></div>

</p>

		</div></li>

			<div class="thumb" style="width: 244.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:St_Patrick%27s_Cathedral_-_Gothic_Revival_Style.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/St_Patrick%27s_Cathedral_-_Gothic_Revival_Style.jpg/367px-St_Patrick%27s_Cathedral_-_Gothic_Revival_Style.jpg" decoding="async" width="245" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/St_Patrick%27s_Cathedral_-_Gothic_Revival_Style.jpg/551px-St_Patrick%27s_Cathedral_-_Gothic_Revival_Style.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/St_Patrick%27s_Cathedral_-_Gothic_Revival_Style.jpg/735px-St_Patrick%27s_Cathedral_-_Gothic_Revival_Style.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7772" data-file-height="6347" /></a></div></div>

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>In the 19th century the Gothic style of church architecture was transported to Latin America by the Spanish and Portuguese, where it became a symbol of Christian values and traditions. It was also exported to Asia, largely for the churches of the European settlements there. The English Gothic style was a major influence in Australia, both for churches and civic buildings. <a href="/wiki/Perth_Town_Hall" title="Perth Town Hall">Perth Town Hall</a> (1867–70) was the only town hall in Australia (or perhaps anywhere) constructed by convicts.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_the_Twenty-Six_Holy_Martyrs_of_Japan_(Nagasaki)" title="Basilica of the Twenty-Six Holy Martyrs of Japan (Nagasaki)">Basilica of the Twenty-Six Holy Martyrs</a> (1879) was an enlargement of the first Christian church in Japan, which had been built in 1864. It survived the dropping of an atomic bomb on <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki" title="Nagasaki">Nagasaki</a> in August 1945. The plan was taken from a Belgian model, and the stained glass was imported from France.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Templo_Expiatorio_del_Sant%C3%ADsimo_Sacramento" title="Templo Expiatorio del Santísimo Sacramento">Church of the Holy Sacrament</a> in <a href="/wiki/Guadalajara" title="Guadalajara">Guadalajara</a>, Mexico was begun in 1897, but construction was halted by the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Revolution" title="Mexican Revolution">Mexican Revolution</a>, and it was not completed until 1972.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/St._Ignatius_Cathedral" title="St. Ignatius Cathedral">Shanghai Cathedral</a> in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (1906–1910) was originally built by the <a href="/wiki/Jesuits" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesuits">Jesuits</a> as their primary church in the trading port of Shanghai. In 1966, at the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_Guards_(China)" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Guards (China)">Red Guards</a> from Beijing vandalized the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral" title="Cathedral">cathedral</a>, tearing down its <a href="/wiki/Spire" title="Spire">spires</a> and ceiling, and smashing its roughly 300 square meters of stained glass. For the next ten years the Cathedral served as a state-owned grain warehouse. It was reopened in 1978 and the spires were restored in the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> has a number of churches with Gothic architecture, all of which built by the <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spanish</a> who were until 1898 were in control of the archipelago as a colony. Examples are the <a href="/wiki/San_Sebastian_Church_(Manila)" title="San Sebastian Church (Manila)">Basilica of San Sebastian</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manila" title="Manila">Manila</a> completed in 1891 and the <a href="/wiki/Molo,_Iloilo_City" title="Molo, Iloilo City">Parish of Saint Anne</a> in <a href="/wiki/Iloilo_City" title="Iloilo City">Iloilo</a> which was completed in 1795 but was rebuilt in its present Gothic design in 1831. The main church in <a href="/wiki/Davao_City" title="Davao City">Davao</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Davao_Cathedral" title="Davao Cathedral">Saint Peter Cathedral</a>, was also originally Gothic in design by the time it was consecrated in 1848, but was redesigned in order to make room for more church-goers in the 1960s and was given its present <a href="/wiki/Noah%27s_Ark" title="Noah&#39;s Ark">ark</a>-shaped design.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In India, the city of <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a> (formerly Bombay) has a rich collection of neo-Gothic architecture. The style is found in the University buildings, the courthouse, and in the <a href="/wiki/Chhatrapati_Shivaji_Terminus_railway_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Chhatrapati Shivaji Terminus railway station">Chhatrapati Shivaji Terminus railway station</a> (former Victoria Terminus), the main railway station.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Division_of_interior_space">Division of interior space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Division of interior space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Another characteristic feature of the Gothic style, domestic and ecclesiastical alike, is the division of interior space into individual cells according to the building's ribbing and vaults, regardless of whether or not the structure actually has a vaulted ceiling. This system of cells of varying size and shape juxtaposed in various patterns was again totally unique to antiquity and the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a> and scholars, Frankl included, have emphasised the mathematical and geometric nature of this design. Frankl in particular thought of this layout as "creation by division" rather than the <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a>'s "creation by addition." Others, namely <a href="/wiki/Viollet-le-Duc" class="mw-redirect" title="Viollet-le-Duc">Viollet-le-Duc</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Wilhelm_Pinder&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Wilhelm Pinder (page does not exist)">Wilhelm Pinder</a>, and <a href="/wiki/August_Schmarsow" title="August Schmarsow">August Schmarsow</a>, instead proposed the term "articulated architecture."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197714_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197714-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The opposite theory as suggested by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Focillon" title="Henri Focillon">Henri Focillon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean_Bony" title="Jean Bony">Jean Bony</a> is of "spacial unification", or of the creation of an interior that is made for sensory overload via the interaction of many elements and perspectives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197714,_17_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197714,_17-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Interior and exterior partitions, often extensively studied, have been found to at times contain features, such as thoroughfares at window height, that make the illusion of thickness. Additionally, the <a href="/wiki/Pier_(architecture)" title="Pier (architecture)">piers</a> separating the isles eventually stopped being part of the walls but rather independent objects that jut out from the actual aisle wall itself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197717_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197717-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Romanesque_and_Norman_influence">Romanesque and Norman influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Romanesque and Norman influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 312.66666666667px"><div style="width: 312.66666666667px">

Early Gothic rib vaults are combined with round arches and other Romanesque features.
</p>

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque architecture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norman_architecture" title="Norman architecture">Norman architecture</a> had a major influence upon Gothic architecture. The plan of the Gothic cathedral was based upon the plan of the ancient Roman <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilica</a>, which was adopted by Romanesque architecture. The <a href="/wiki/Latin_cross" title="Latin cross">Latin cross</a> form, with a <a href="/wiki/Nave" title="Nave">nave</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transept" title="Transept">transept</a>, choir, disambulatory, and radiating chapels, came from the Romanesque model. The grand arcades of columns separating the central vessel of the <a href="/wiki/Nave" title="Nave">nave</a> from the collateral aisles, the <a href="/wiki/Triforium" title="Triforium">Triforium</a> over the grand arcades, and the windows high on the walls allowing light into the nave were all also adapted from the Romanesque model. The portal with a <a href="/wiki/Tympanum_(architecture)" title="Tympanum (architecture)">tympanum</a> filled with sculpture was another characteristic Romanesque feature, as was the use of the buttress to support the walls from the outside. Gothic architects improved them by adding the <a href="/wiki/Flying_buttress" title="Flying buttress">flying buttress</a> with high arches connecting the buttresses to the upper walls. In the interior, Romanesque architecture used the <a href="/wiki/Barrel_vault" title="Barrel vault">barrel vault</a> with a round arch to cover the nave, and a <a href="/wiki/Groin_vault" title="Groin vault">groin vault</a> when two barrel vaults met at right angles. These vaults were the immediate ancestors of the Gothic <a href="/wiki/Rib_vault" title="Rib vault">rib vault</a>. The first use of the Gothic rib vaults to cover a nave was in the Romanesque <a href="/wiki/Durham_Cathedral" title="Durham Cathedral">Durham Cathedral</a>, (1093-1104).<sup id="cite_ref-auto_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Norman_Architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Architecture">Norman Architecture</a>, similar to the Romanesque style, also influenced the Gothic style. Early examples are found in <a href="/wiki/Lessay_Abbey" title="Lessay Abbey">Lessay Abbey</a> in Normandy, which also featured early rib vaults in the nave similar to the Gothic vaults. <a href="/wiki/Cefalu_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Cefalu Cathedral">Cefalu Cathedral</a> (1131-1267) in Sicily, built when Sicily was under Norman rule, is another interesting example. It featured pointed arches and large Romanesque vaults combined with ornamental mosaic decoration.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque architecture</a> had become a pan-European style and manner of construction, affecting buildings in countries as far apart as <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>. The same wide geographic area was then affected by the development of Gothic architecture, but the acceptance of the Gothic style and methods of construction differed from place to place, as did the expressions of Gothic taste. The proximity of some regions meant that modern country borders did not define divisions of style. Many different factors like geographical/geological, economic, social, or political situations caused the regional differences in the great abbey churches and cathedrals of the Romanesque period that would often become even more apparent in the Gothic. For example, studies of the population statistics reveals disparities such as the multitude of churches, abbeys, and cathedrals in northern <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> while in more urbanised regions construction activity of a similar scale was reserved to a few important cities. Such an example comes from Roberto López, wherein the French city of <a href="/wiki/Amiens" title="Amiens">Amiens</a> was able to fund its architectural projects whereas <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> could not because of the economic inequality of the two.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197725-26_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197725-26-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> This wealth, concentrated in rich monasteries and noble families, would eventually spread certain Italian, Catalan, and Hanseatic bankers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197725_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197725-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> This would be amended when the economic hardships of the 13th century were no longer felt, allowing <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">Tuscany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a>, and the southern <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">Rhineland</a> to enter into competition with France.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197726_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197726-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<p>The pointed arch, one of the defining attributes of Gothic, was earlier featured in <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic architecture</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-BF_19-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Though it did not have the same functions. Precursor of pointed arch appeared in <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_architecture" title="Sasanian architecture">Sassanian</a> architectures, This was evidenced in early church building in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and occasional secular structures, like the <a href="/wiki/Karamagara_Bridge" title="Karamagara Bridge">Karamagara Bridge</a>; in Sassanian architecture, employed in palace and sacred construction. These pre-Islamic arches were decorative rather than structural in their function.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Warren1991_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warren1991-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The pointed arch as an architectonic principle was first clearly established in Islamic architecture; as an architectonic principle, the pointed arch was entirely alien to the pre-Islamic world.<sup id="cite_ref-Bloom_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloom-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Use of the pointed arch seems to have taken off dramatically in Islamic architecture. It begins to appear throughout the Islamic world in close succession after its adoption in the late Umayyad or early Abbasid period. Some examples are the <a href="/wiki/Al-Ukhaidir_Fortress" title="Al-Ukhaidir Fortress">Al-Ukhaidir Palace</a> (775 AD), the Abbasid reconstruction of the <a href="/wiki/Al-Aqsa_Mosque" title="Al-Aqsa Mosque">Al-Aqsa mosque</a> in 780 AD, the <a href="/wiki/White_Mosque,_Ramla" title="White Mosque, Ramla">Ramlah Cisterns</a> (789 AD), the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Samarra" title="Great Mosque of Samarra">Great Mosque of Samarra</a> (851 AD), and the <a href="/wiki/Mosque_of_Ibn_Tulun" title="Mosque of Ibn Tulun">Mosque of Ibn Tulun</a> (879 AD) in Cairo. It also appears in one of the early reconstructions of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan" title="Great Mosque of Kairouan">Great Mosque of Kairouan</a> in Tunisia, and the Mosque–Cathedral of Córdoba in 987 AD. The pointed arch had already been used in Syria, but in the mosque of Ibn Tulun we have one of the earliest examples of its use on an extensive scale, some centuries before it was exploited in the West by the Gothic architects.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A kind of <a href="/wiki/Rib_vault" title="Rib vault">rib vault</a> was also used in Islamic architecture, for example in the ceiling of the <a href="/wiki/Mosque-Cathedral_of_Cordoba" class="mw-redirect" title="Mosque-Cathedral of Cordoba">Mosque-Cathedral of Cordoba</a>. In Cordoba, the dome was supported by <a href="/wiki/Pendentives" class="mw-redirect" title="Pendentives">pendentives</a>, which connected the dome to the arches below. The pendentives were decorated with ribs.  Unlike the Gothic rib vault, the Islamic ribs were purely decorative;  they did not extend outside of the vault, and they were not part of the structure supporting the roof. 
</p><p>The military and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_world_contributions_to_Medieval_Europe" title="Islamic world contributions to Medieval Europe">cultural contacts with</a> the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Islamic_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval Islamic world">medieval Islamic world</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_southern_Italy" title="Norman conquest of southern Italy">Norman conquest</a> of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam_in_southern_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Islam in southern Italy">Islamic Sicily</a> in 1090, the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a> (beginning 1096), and the <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Islamic presence in Spain</a>, may have influenced <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval Europe">Medieval Europe</a>'s adoption of the pointed arch.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott2003113_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott2003113-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBony198317_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBony198317-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Another feature of Gothic architecutre, the rib vault, had also earlier appeared in Islamic architecture, and spread to Western Europe via <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Islamic Spain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Sicily" title="Emirate of Sicily">Sicily</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bloom_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloom-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Giese-Vogeli_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giese-Vogeli-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The early rib vaults in Spain were used to support cupolas, and were decorative. The dome of the Mosque-Cathedral of Cordoba was supported by <a href="/wiki/Pendentive" title="Pendentive">Pendentives</a>, rather than the vault. These were frequently used in <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine architecture</a>, as in the dome of <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a> in Istanbul, which also was supported by pendentives. The Gothic rib vault, amongst other features, such as the flying buttress, have their antecedents in Romanesque architecture such as <a href="/wiki/Durham_Cathedral" title="Durham Cathedral">Durham Cathedral</a>, constructed between 1093 and 1096.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott2003113_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott2003113-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMignon201510_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMignon201510-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In those parts of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Mediterranean">Western Mediterranean</a> subject to Islamic control or influence, rich regional variants arose, fusing <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> and later Gothic traditions with <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic</a> decorative forms. For example, in <a href="/wiki/Monreale_Cathedral" title="Monreale Cathedral">Monreale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cefal%C3%B9_Cathedral" title="Cefalù Cathedral">Cefalù Cathedrals</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Alc%C3%A1zar_of_Seville" title="Alcázar of Seville">Alcázar of Seville</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Teruel_Cathedral" title="Teruel Cathedral">Teruel Cathedral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of scholars have cited the <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenian</a> <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Ani" title="Cathedral of Ani">Cathedral of Ani</a>, completed 1001 or 1010, as a possible influence on the Gothic, especially due to its use of pointed arches and cluster <a href="/wiki/Pier_(architecture)" title="Pier (architecture)">piers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELang1980223_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELang1980223-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart195980_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart195980-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERice1972179_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERice1972179-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> However, other scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Sirarpie_Der_Nersessian" title="Sirarpie Der Nersessian">Sirarpie Der Nersessian</a>, who rejected this notion as she argued that the pointed arches did not serve the same function of supporting the vault.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGarsoïan2015300_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGarsoïan2015300-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Lucy Der Manuelian contends that some <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a> (historically documented as being in Western Europe in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197737_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197737-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> could have brought the knowledge and technique employed at Ani to the west.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDer_Manuelian20017_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDer_Manuelian20017-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political">Political</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Political">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The roots of the Gothic style lie in those towns that, since the 11th century, had been enjoying increased prosperity and growth alongside more and more freedom from traditional <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudal</a> authority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchell19689_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchell19689-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> At the end of the 12th century, Europe was divided into a multitude of <a href="/wiki/City_state" class="mw-redirect" title="City state">city states</a> and kingdoms. The area encompassing modern <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> and much of northern <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> (excluding <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Papal_State" class="mw-redirect" title="Papal State">Papal State</a>) was nominally part of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, but local rulers exercised considerable autonomy under the system of <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">Feudalism</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_France" title="Kingdom of France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1025%E2%80%931385)" title="Kingdom of Poland (1025–1385)">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary" title="Kingdom of Hungary">Hungary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Portugal" title="Kingdom of Portugal">Portugal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Castile" title="Kingdom of Castile">Castile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Aragon" title="Crown of Aragon">Aragon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Navarre" title="Kingdom of Navarre">Navarre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sicily" title="Kingdom of Sicily">Sicily</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Cyprus" title="Kingdom of Cyprus">Cyprus</a> were independent kingdoms, as was the <a href="/wiki/Angevin_Empire" title="Angevin Empire">Angevin Empire</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/House_of_Plantagenet" title="House of Plantagenet">Plantagenet kings</a> ruled <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a> and large domains in what was to become modern <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> came under the influence of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, while the other Scandinavian countries and <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> were influenced by trading contacts with the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>. <a href="/wiki/Capetian_House_of_Anjou" title="Capetian House of Anjou">Angevin</a> kings brought the Gothic tradition from France to <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">Southern Italy</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Lusignan" class="mw-redirect" title="Lusignan">Lusignan</a> kings introduced <a href="/wiki/French_Gothic" class="mw-redirect" title="French Gothic">French Gothic</a> architecture to <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>. Gothic art is sometimes viewed as the art of the era of feudalism but also as being connected to change in medieval social structure, as the Gothic style of architecture seemed to parallel the beginning of the decline of feudalism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197720,_24_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197720,_24-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, the influence of the established feudal elite can be seen in the <a href="/wiki/Chateaux" class="mw-redirect" title="Chateaux">Chateaux</a> of French lords and in those churches sponsored by feudal lords.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197724_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197724-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout Europe at this time there was a rapid growth in trade and an associated growth in towns,<sup id="cite_ref-BF_19-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JH1_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JH1-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> and they would come to be predominate in Europe by the end of the 13th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197724_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197724-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Germany and the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a> had large flourishing towns that grew in comparative peace, in trade and competition with each other or united for mutual weal, as in the Hanseatic League. Civic buildings were of great importance to these towns since they served as signs of wealth and pride. England and France remained largely feudal and produced grand domestic architecture for their kings, dukes and <a href="/wiki/Bishops" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishops">bishops</a>, rather than grand <a href="/wiki/Town_hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Town hall">town halls</a> for their <a href="/wiki/Medieval_bourgeoisie" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval bourgeoisie">burghers</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Viollet-le-Duc contended that the blossoming of the Gothic style came about as a result of growing freedoms in construction professions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197724_121-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197724-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The primary use of the Gothic style is in religious structures, naturally leading it to an association with the Church and it is considered to be one of the most formal and coordinated forms of the physical church, thought of as being the physical residence of God on Earth. According to <a href="/wiki/Hans_Sedlmayr" title="Hans Sedlmayr">Hans Sedlmayr</a>, it was "even the considered the temporal image of <a href="/wiki/Paradise" title="Paradise">Paradise</a>, of the <a href="/wiki/New_Jerusalem" title="New Jerusalem">New Jerusalem</a>." The horizontal and vertical scope of the Gothic church, filled with the light thought of as a symbol of the grace of God admitted into the structure via the style's iconic windows are among the very best examples of Christian architecture. <a href="/wiki/Louis_Grodecki" title="Louis Grodecki">Grodecki</a>'s <i><a href="#CITEREFGrodecki1977">Gothic Architecture</a></i> also notes that the glass pieces of various colors that make up those windows have been compared to "precious stones encrusting the walls of the New Jerusalem," and that "the numerous towers and pinnacles evoke similar structures that appear in the visions of <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">Saint John</a>." Another idea, held by <a href="/wiki/Georg_Dehio" title="Georg Dehio">Georg Dehio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Panofsky" title="Erwin Panofsky">Erwin Panofsky</a>, is that the designs of Gothic followed the current theological scholastic thought.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197720_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197720-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a> show <a href="/wiki/Nova_(American_TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nova (American TV series)">NOVA</a> explored the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> in the dimensions and design of some cathedrals.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Materials">Materials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Materials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The local availability of materials affected both construction and style. In <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> was readily available in several grades, the very fine white limestone of <a href="/wiki/Caen" title="Caen">Caen</a> being favoured for sculptural decoration. England had coarse limestone and red <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> as well as dark green <a href="/wiki/Purbeck_marble" class="mw-redirect" title="Purbeck marble">Purbeck marble</a> which was often used for architectural features. In <a href="/wiki/Northern_Germany" title="Northern Germany">northern Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, and the Baltic countries local building stone was unavailable but there was a strong tradition of building in brick. The resultant style, <a href="/wiki/Brick_Gothic" title="Brick Gothic">Brick Gothic</a> – called <i lang="pl" title="Polish language text">Gotyk ceglany</i> in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> and <i lang="de" title="German language text">Backsteingotik</i> in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> – is also associated with the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, stone was used for <a href="/wiki/Fortifications" class="mw-redirect" title="Fortifications">fortifications</a>, so brick was preferred for other buildings. Because of the extensive and varied deposits of marble, many buildings were faced in marble, or were left with undecorated façade so that this might be achieved at a later date. The availability of timber also influenced the style of architecture, with timber buildings prevailing in <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>. Availability of timber affected methods of roof construction across Europe. It is thought that the magnificent <a href="/wiki/Hammerbeam" class="mw-redirect" title="Hammerbeam">hammerbeam</a> roofs of England were devised as a direct response to the lack of long straight seasoned timber by the end of the Medieval period, when forests had been decimated not only for the construction of vast roofs but also for ship building.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_19-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CT_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CT-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

Historic relationships between the two countries played a determining role: in 1154, Henry II (1154–1189) became the first of the Anjou Plantagenet kings to ascend to the throne of England."</span>

<li id="cite_note-110"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-110">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Harvey, L. P. (1992). "Islamic Spain, 1250 to 1500." Chicago&#160;: University of Chicago Press. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-226-31960-1" title="Special:BookSources/0-226-31960-1">0-226-31960-1</a>; Boswell, John (1978). <li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTELang1980223-111"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTELang1980223_111-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFLang1980">Lang 1980</a>, p.&#160;223: "With this experience behind him, it is not surprising that <a href="/wiki/Trdat_the_Architect" class="mw-redirect" title="Trdat the Architect">Trdat</a>'s creation of the Cathedral at <a href="/wiki/Ani" title="Ani">Ani</a> turned out to be a masterpiece. Even without its dome, the cathedral amazes the onlooker. Technically, it is far ahead of the contemporary <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_architecture" title="Anglo-Saxon architecture">Anglo-Saxon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norman_architecture" title="Norman architecture">Norman architecture</a> of Europe. Already, pointed arches and clustered piers, whose appearance together is considered one of the hallmarks of mature Gothic architecture, are found in this remote corner of the Christian East."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-112"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-112">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Kite, Stephen (September 2003). "<span class="cs1-kern-left">'</span>South Opposed to East and North': Adrian Stokes and Josef Strzygowski. A study in the aesthetics and historiography of Orientalism". <i><a href="/wiki/Art_History_(journal)" title="Art History (journal)">Art History</a></i>. <b>26</b> (4): 505–533. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1111%2Fj.0141-6790.2003.02604002.x">10.1111/j.0141-6790.2003.02604002.x</a>. <q>To Near Eastern scholars the <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenian</a> <a href="/wiki/Ani_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Ani Cathedral">cathedral at</a> <a href="/wiki/Ani" title="Ani">Ani</a> (989–1001), designed by <a href="/wiki/Trdat_the_Architect" class="mw-redirect" title="Trdat the Architect">Trdat</a> (972–1036), seemed to anticipate Gothic.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Art+History&amp;rft.atitle=%27South+Opposed+to+East+and+North%27%3A+Adrian+Stokes+and+Josef+Strzygowski.+A+study+in+the+aesthetics+and+historiography+of+Orientalism&amp;rft.volume=26&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.pages=505-533&amp;rft.date=2003-09&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1111%2Fj.0141-6790.2003.02604002.x&amp;rft.aulast=Kite&amp;rft.aufirst=Stephen&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGothic+architecture" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart195980-113"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart195980_113-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFStewart1959">Stewart 1959</a>, p.&#160;80: "The most important examples of <a href="/wiki/Armenian_architecture" title="Armenian architecture">Armenian architecture</a> are to be found at <a href="/wiki/Ani" title="Ani">Ani</a>, the capital, and the most important of these is <a href="/wiki/Ani_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Ani Cathedral">the cathedral</a>. [...] The most interesting features of this building are its pointed arches and vaults and the clustering or coupling of the columns in the Gothic manner."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTERice1972179-114"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTERice1972179_114-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFRice1972">Rice 1972</a>, p.&#160;179: "The interior of <a href="/wiki/Ani_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Ani Cathedral">Ani cathedral</a>, a longitudinal stone building with pointed vaults and a central dome, built about 1001, is astonishingly Gothic in every detail, and numerous other equally close parallels could be cited."</span>
</li>
